Wisse Rutgers acknowledges financial support from the Fundaci√≥n Ram√≥n Areces, the Maria de Maeztu Unit of Excellence CEX2020-001104-M, funded by MCIN/AEI/10.13039/501100011033, and CEMFI."):

|  |  |  |
| --- | --- | --- |
|  | MPE=ùîº0‚Äã[Œ®ifixed‚ÄãsW‚Äã(Wi)]+‚ü®Œ®conduct,sR‚ü©‚ÑãR.\text{MPE}=\mathbb{E}\_{0}[\Psi\_{i}^{\text{fixed}}s\_{W}(W\_{i})]+\langle\Psi^{\text{conduct}},s\_{R}\rangle\_{\mathcal{H}\_{R}}. |  |

1. 1.

   Fixed Component Œ®ifixed\Psi\_{i}^{\text{fixed}}: This component is identical to the previous auction example: Œ®ifixed=Yi+Œ≥‚Äã(Ri)\Psi\_{i}^{\text{fixed}}=Y\_{i}+\gamma(R\_{i}).
2. 2.

   Market Conduct Component ‚ü®Œ®conduct,sR‚ü©‚ÑãR\langle\Psi^{\text{conduct}},s\_{R}\rangle\_{\mathcal{H}\_{R}}: This term is ‚ü®‚àácùí∞‚Äã(c0)‚ãÖœàc0‚Äã(Ri),sR‚Äã(Ri)‚ü©H1\langle\nabla\_{c}\mathcal{U}(c\_{0})\cdot\psi\_{c\_{0}}(R\_{i}),s\_{R}(R\_{i})\rangle\_{H^{1}}. We derive the representer œàc0\psi\_{c\_{0}} below.

   * ‚Ä¢

     Welfare Gradient ‚àácùí∞‚Äã(c0)\nabla\_{c}\mathcal{U}(c\_{0}): This is identical to the previous auction example:

     |  |  |  |
     | --- | --- | --- |
     |  | ‚àácùí∞‚Äã(c0)=‚àíœÑ‚Äã(c0)‚ÄãfR|0‚Äã(c0)‚ÄãFR|0‚Äã(c0)n‚àí1.\nabla\_{c}\mathcal{U}(c\_{0})=-\tau(c\_{0})f\_{R|0}(c\_{0})F\_{R|0}(c\_{0})^{n-1}. |  |
   * ‚Ä¢

     Characterization of the Representer œàc0\psi\_{c\_{0}}: The representer is defined by the relation c‚Ä≤‚Äã[sR]=‚ü®œàc0,sR‚ü©H1c^{\prime}[s\_{R}]=\langle\psi\_{c\_{0}},s\_{R}\rangle\_{H^{1}}. We first find an expression for the functional c‚Ä≤‚Äã[sR]c^{\prime}[s\_{R}] using the Implicit Function Theorem on G‚Äã(c,PR)=0G(c,P\_{R})=0. Differentiating w.r.t. the policy perturbation at baseline gives:

     |  |  |  |
     | --- | --- | --- |
     |  | ‚àÇG‚àÇc|c0,PR|0‚ãÖc‚Ä≤‚Äã[sR]+‚àÇG‚àÇPR‚Äã[sR]|c0,PR|0=0.\frac{\partial G}{\partial c}\bigg|\_{c\_{0},P\_{R|0}}\cdot c^{\prime}[s\_{R}]+\frac{\partial G}{\partial P\_{R}}[s\_{R}]\bigg|\_{c\_{0},P\_{R|0}}=0. |  |

     The partial derivatives are ‚àÇG‚àÇc=‚àí2‚ÄãfR‚Äã(c)‚àíc‚ÄãfR‚Ä≤‚Äã(c)\frac{\partial G}{\partial c}=-2f\_{R}(c)-cf^{\prime}\_{R}(c) and
     ‚àÇG‚àÇPR‚Äã[sR]=‚àíùîº0‚Äã[sR‚Äã(Ri)‚Äãùüè‚Äã{Ri‚â§c0}]‚àíc0‚ÄãfR|0‚Äã(c0)‚ÄãsR‚Äã(c0)\frac{\partial G}{\partial P\_{R}}[s\_{R}]=-\mathbb{E}\_{0}[s\_{R}(R\_{i})\mathbf{1}\{R\_{i}\leq c\_{0}\}]-c\_{0}f\_{R|0}(c\_{0})s\_{R}(c\_{0}).
     Solving for c‚Ä≤‚Äã[sR]c^{\prime}[s\_{R}] gives the functional:

     |  |  |  |
     | --- | --- | --- |
     |  | c‚Ä≤‚Äã[sR]=K‚ãÖ(ùîº0‚Äã[sR‚Äã(Ri)‚Äãùüè‚Äã{Ri‚â§c0}]+c0‚ÄãfR|0‚Äã(c0)‚ÄãsR‚Äã(c0)),c^{\prime}[s\_{R}]=K\cdot\left(\mathbb{E}\_{0}[s\_{R}(R\_{i})\mathbf{1}\{R\_{i}\leq c\_{0}\}]+c\_{0}f\_{R|0}(c\_{0})s\_{R}(c\_{0})\right), |  |

     where K=(2‚ÄãfR|0‚Äã(c0)+c0‚ÄãfR|0‚Ä≤‚Äã(c0))‚àí1K=(2f\_{R|0}(c\_{0})+c\_{0}f^{\prime}\_{R|0}(c\_{0}))^{-1}. We now equate this with the inner product form:

     |  |  |  |
     | --- | --- | --- |
     |  | ùîº0‚Äã[œàc0‚ÄãsR]+ùîº0‚Äã[œàc0‚Ä≤‚ÄãsR‚Ä≤]=K‚ãÖ(ùîº0‚Äã[sR‚ãÖùüè‚â§c0]+c0‚ÄãfR|0‚Äã(c0)‚ÄãsR‚Äã(c0)).\mathbb{E}\_{0}[\psi\_{c\_{0}}s\_{R}]+\mathbb{E}\_{0}[\psi^{\prime}\_{c\_{0}}s^{\prime}\_{R}]=K\cdot\left(\mathbb{E}\_{0}[s\_{R}\cdot\mathbf{1}\_{\leq c\_{0}}]+c\_{0}f\_{R|0}(c\_{0})s\_{R}(c\_{0})\right). |  |

     Using integration by parts on the second term on the left, ùîº0‚Äã[œàc0‚Ä≤‚ÄãsR‚Ä≤]=‚àíùîº0‚Äã[sR‚Äã(œàc0‚Ä≤‚ÄãfR|0)‚Ä≤/fR|0]\mathbb{E}\_{0}[\psi^{\prime}\_{c\_{0}}s^{\prime}\_{R}]=-\mathbb{E}\_{0}[s\_{R}(\psi^{\prime}\_{c\_{0}}f\_{R|0})^{\prime}/f\_{R|0}], and representing the point evaluation using the Dirac delta function, sR‚Äã(c0)=ùîº0‚Äã[sR‚Äã(Ri)‚ÄãŒ¥‚Äã(Ri‚àíc0)/fR|0‚Äã(c0)]s\_{R}(c\_{0})=\mathbb{E}\_{0}[s\_{R}(R\_{i})\delta(R\_{i}-c\_{0})/f\_{R|0}(c\_{0})], we can group all terms under a single expectation over sR‚Äã(Ri)s\_{R}(R\_{i}):

     |  |  |  |
     | --- | --- | --- |
     |  | ùîº0‚Äã[sR‚Äã(Ri)‚Äã(œàc0‚Äã(Ri)‚àí(œàc0‚Ä≤‚Äã(Ri)‚ÄãfR|0‚Äã(Ri))‚Ä≤fR|0‚Äã(Ri)‚àíK‚ãÖ(ùüè‚Äã{Ri‚â§c0}+c0‚ÄãŒ¥‚Äã(Ri‚àíc0)))]=0.\mathbb{E}\_{0}\left[s\_{R}(R\_{i})\left(\psi\_{c\_{0}}(R\_{i})-\frac{(\psi^{\prime}\_{c\_{0}}(R\_{i})f\_{R|0}(R\_{i}))^{\prime}}{f\_{R|0}(R\_{i})}-K\cdot(\mathbf{1}\{R\_{i}\leq c\_{0}\}+c\_{0}\delta(R\_{i}-c\_{0}))\right)\right]=0. |  |

     Since this must hold for all sR‚ààH1s\_{R}\in H^{1}, the term in the parentheses must be zero. This yields the Sturm-Liouville differential equation that uniquely defines the representer œàc0‚Äã(r)\psi\_{c\_{0}}(r):

     |  |  |  |
     | --- | --- | --- |
     |  | œàc0‚Äã(r)‚ÄãfR|0‚Äã(r)‚àí(œàc0‚Ä≤‚Äã(r)‚ÄãfR|0‚Äã(r))‚Ä≤=K‚ãÖ(ùüè‚Äã{r‚â§c0}‚ÄãfR|0‚Äã(r)+c0‚ÄãfR|0‚Äã(c0)‚ÄãŒ¥‚Äã(r‚àíc0)).\psi\_{c\_{0}}(r)f\_{R|0}(r)-(\psi^{\prime}\_{c\_{0}}(r)f\_{R|0}(r))^{\prime}=K\cdot\left(\mathbf{1}\{r\leq c\_{0}\}f\_{R|0}(r)+c\_{0}f\_{R|0}(c\_{0})\delta(r-c\_{0})\right). |  |
3. 3.

   Total Marginal Policy Effect:
   The MPE is therefore:

   |  |  |  |
   | --- | --- | --- |
   |  | MPE=ùîº0‚Äã[(Yi+Œ≥‚Äã(Ri))‚ÄãsW‚Äã(Wi)]+‚àácùí∞‚Äã(c0)‚ãÖ‚ü®œàc0,sR‚ü©H1.\displaystyle\text{MPE}=\mathbb{E}\_{0}[(Y\_{i}+\gamma(R\_{i}))s\_{W}(W\_{i})]+\nabla\_{c}\mathcal{U}(c\_{0})\cdot\langle\psi\_{c\_{0}},s\_{R}\rangle\_{H^{1}}. |  |

   This expression cannot be simplified into a single covariance. It demonstrates that when the market-clearing rule is itself the solution to an optimization problem that depends on local features of the report distribution, the welfare impact of a policy reform depends not just on its direction (sRs\_{R}), but also on its smoothness (via the derivative term sR‚Ä≤s^{\prime}\_{R} implicit in the H1H^{1} inner product).

### C.5 Top Trading Cycles

This section discusses the Top Trading Cycles (TTC) mechanism. Unlike the previous examples, the market-clearing parameters (the cutoffs) are defined as the solution to a dynamic system. We first describe this characterization and then provide a high-level sketch of our analysis.

#### C.5.1 Model Specification and Cutoff Characterization

We follow the continuum model of Leshno and Lo ([2021](https://arxiv.org/html/2510.20032v1#bib.bib31)). The market consists of a continuum of students and a finite set of schools ùíû={1,‚Ä¶,n}\mathcal{C}=\{1,...,n\} with capacities qcq\_{c}. A student‚Äôs type RiR\_{i} includes their strict preference ordering ‚âªi\succ\_{i} and a vector of priority scores Vi=(Vi,1,‚Ä¶,Vi,n)‚àà[0,1]nV\_{i}=(V\_{i,1},...,V\_{i,n})\in[0,1]^{n}.

* ‚Ä¢

  Allocation Rule and Cutoffs: The TTC algorithm assigns students by clearing trading cycles. Leshno and Lo ([2021](https://arxiv.org/html/2510.20032v1#bib.bib31)) show that the final assignment can be described by a matrix of cutoffs ùêú={ca,b}a,b‚ààùíû\mathbf{c}=\{c\_{a,b}\}\_{a,b\in\mathcal{C}}. A student ii is admitted to their most-preferred school aa within their "budget set," which is the set of schools B‚Äã(Ri,ùêú)={k‚ààùíû‚à£‚àÉb‚ààùíû‚Äã¬†s.t.¬†‚ÄãVi,b‚â•ck,b}B(R\_{i},\mathbf{c})=\{k\in\mathcal{C}\mid\exists b\in\mathcal{C}\text{ s.t. }V\_{i,b}\geq c\_{k,b}\}
* ‚Ä¢

  Market Conduct Rule (Dynamic System): The cutoff matrix ùêú\mathbf{c} is not determined by a simple set of algebraic equations but as the solution to a dynamic process. The key objects are:

  1. 1.

     The TTC Path Œ≥‚Äã(t)\bm{\gamma}(t): A vector-valued function ùú∏‚Äã(t)‚àà[0,1]n\bm{\gamma}(t)\in[0,1]^{n} that tracks the priority frontiers of the schools over time, starting from ùú∏‚Äã(0)=ùüè\bm{\gamma}(0)=\mathbf{1}.
  2. 2.

     Marginal Trade Balance Equations: The path evolves according to a system of ordinary differential equations that ensure the "flow" of students trading into a school equals the "flow" of students trading out of it at every moment. For each school kk, this is:

     |  |  |  |
     | --- | --- | --- |
     |  | ‚àëb‚ààùíûŒ≥b‚Ä≤‚Äã(t)‚ÄãHbk‚Äã(ùú∏‚Äã(t))=‚àëa‚ààùíûŒ≥k‚Ä≤‚Äã(t)‚ÄãHka‚Äã(ùú∏‚Äã(t)).\sum\_{b\in\mathcal{C}}\gamma^{\prime}\_{b}(t)H\_{b}^{k}(\bm{\gamma}(t))=\sum\_{a\in\mathcal{C}}\gamma^{\prime}\_{k}(t)H\_{k}^{a}(\bm{\gamma}(t)). |  |

     Here, Hbk‚Äã(ùê±)H\_{b}^{k}(\mathbf{x}) is the marginal density of students at the priority frontier ùê±\mathbf{x} whose top choice is school kk and who have the highest priority at school bb.
  3. 3.

     Capacity Equations (Stopping Conditions): The process for a school kk stops at time t(k)t^{(k)} when its capacity is filled. The final cutoffs are determined by the path evaluated at these stopping times: ck,b=Œ≥b‚Äã(t(k))c\_{k,b}=\gamma\_{b}(t^{(k)}).

The market conduct rule ùêú‚Äã(PR)\mathbf{c}(P\_{R}) is therefore the function that maps a distribution of reports PRP\_{R} (which determines the marginal densities HbkH\_{b}^{k}) to the matrix of cutoffs that solves this dynamic system.

#### C.5.2 TTC in the MPE Framework

For a given cutoff matrix ùêú\mathbf{c}, the allocation rule Œºa‚Äã(Ri,ùêú)\mu\_{a}(R\_{i},\mathbf{c}) is a complex but deterministic indicator function. It can be written in our decomposition form with ha=1h\_{a}=1 and œïa‚Äã(Ri,ùêú)\phi\_{a}(R\_{i},\mathbf{c}) representing the condition that aa is the most-preferred school in the budget set B‚Äã(Ri,ùêú)B(R\_{i},\mathbf{c}).
Since ha=1h\_{a}=1, the allocation rule does not depend on PRP\_{R} once ùêú\mathbf{c} is known. Therefore, the Hadamard derivative kernel LaL\_{a} is zero, and the competition externality Œ≥‚Äã(Ri)\gamma(R\_{i}) is zero. The entire equilibrium spillover is captured by the market conduct effect. We assume the primitives are regular enough for the assumptions of Corollary [3.1](https://arxiv.org/html/2510.20032v1#S3.Thmcorollary1 "Corollary 3.1 (The Equilibrium-Adjusted Outcome). ‚Ä£ 3.3 The Equilibrium-Adjusted Outcome ‚Ä£ 3 The Marginal Policy Effect ‚Ä£ Evaluating Local Policies in Centralized MarketsWe thank Kevin Chen, Ramesh Johari, Lihua Lei, Evan Munro, Davide Viviano, and Stefan Wager for their feedback, which has greatly improved the paper. We also thank seminar participants at Harvard and Stanford, as well as conference participants at the 2024 Winter Meetings of the Econometric Society, and ACIC 2025. Wisse Rutgers acknowledges financial support from the Fundaci√≥n Ram√≥n Areces, the Maria de Maeztu Unit of Excellence CEX2020-001104-M, funded by MCIN/AEI/10.13039/501100011033, and CEMFI.") to hold.

#### C.5.3 Derivation for a Parametric Case

Let‚Äôs analyze an economy with two schools (n=2n=2), capacities q1,q2q\_{1},q\_{2}, and a unit mass of students. A fraction œÄ1\pi\_{1} of students prefer school 1, and the remaining œÄ2=1‚àíœÄ1\pi\_{2}=1-\pi\_{1} prefer school 2. Priorities Vi=(Vi,1,Vi,2)V\_{i}=(V\_{i,1},V\_{i,2}) are independently and uniformly distributed on [0,1]2[0,1]^{2} and are independent of preferences.

1. 1.

   Solving the Dynamic System:
   In this setting, the marginal densities are constant: Hbk‚Äã(ùê±)=œÄkH\_{b}^{k}(\mathbf{x})=\pi\_{k} for any b,k‚àà{1,2}b,k\in\{1,2\}. The trade balance equation for school 1 becomes:

   |  |  |  |
   | --- | --- | --- |
   |  | Œ≥1‚Ä≤‚Äã(t)‚ÄãH11+Œ≥2‚Ä≤‚Äã(t)‚ÄãH21=Œ≥1‚Ä≤‚Äã(t)‚ÄãH11+Œ≥1‚Ä≤‚Äã(t)‚ÄãH12‚üπŒ≥2‚Ä≤‚Äã(t)‚ÄãœÄ1=Œ≥1‚Ä≤‚Äã(t)‚ÄãœÄ2.\gamma^{\prime}\_{1}(t)H\_{1}^{1}+\gamma^{\prime}\_{2}(t)H\_{2}^{1}=\gamma^{\prime}\_{1}(t)H\_{1}^{1}+\gamma^{\prime}\_{1}(t)H\_{1}^{2}\implies\gamma^{\prime}\_{2}(t)\pi\_{1}=\gamma^{\prime}\_{1}(t)\pi\_{2}. |  |

   This gives the simple linear ODE Œ≥2‚Ä≤‚Äã(t)/Œ≥1‚Ä≤‚Äã(t)=œÄ2/œÄ1\gamma^{\prime}\_{2}(t)/\gamma^{\prime}\_{1}(t)=\pi\_{2}/\pi\_{1}. Parameterizing the path by tt such that Œ≥1‚Äã(t)=1‚àít\gamma\_{1}(t)=1-t, the solution with initial condition ùú∏‚Äã(0)=(1,1)\bm{\gamma}(0)=(1,1) is the line:

   |  |  |  |
   | --- | --- | --- |
   |  | Œ≥2‚Äã(t)=1‚àí(œÄ2/œÄ1)‚Äãt.\gamma\_{2}(t)=1-(\pi\_{2}/\pi\_{1})t. |  |
2. 2.

   Characterizing the Welfare Gradient ‚àáùêúùí∞\nabla\_{\mathbf{c}}\mathcal{U}:
   The parameter vector ùêú\mathbf{c} is the matrix of four cutoffs {c1,1,c1,2,c2,1,c2,2}\{c\_{1,1},c\_{1,2},c\_{2,1},c\_{2,2}\}. Applying Theorem [A.1](https://arxiv.org/html/2510.20032v1#A1.Thmtheorem1 "Theorem A.1. ‚Ä£ Appendix A Differentiating integrals ‚Ä£ Evaluating Local Policies in Centralized MarketsWe thank Kevin Chen, Ramesh Johari, Lihua Lei, Evan Munro, Davide Viviano, and Stefan Wager for their feedback, which has greatly improved the paper. We also thank seminar participants at Harvard and Stanford, as well as conference participants at the 2024 Winter Meetings of the Econometric Society, and ACIC 2025. Wisse Rutgers acknowledges financial support from the Fundaci√≥n Ram√≥n Areces, the Maria de Maeztu Unit of Excellence CEX2020-001104-M, funded by MCIN/AEI/10.13039/501100011033, and CEMFI."), the gradient ‚àÇùí∞/‚àÇck,b\partial\mathcal{U}/\partial c\_{k,b} is identified by the welfare change of agents on the boundary Vi,b=ck,bV\_{i,b}=c\_{k,b} who are reallocated. We denote this gradient abstractly by ‚àáùêúùí∞\nabla\_{\mathbf{c}}\mathcal{U}.
3. 3.

   Characterizing the Influence Function œàùêú0‚Äã(Ri)\bm{\psi}\_{\mathbf{c}\_{0}}(R\_{i}):
   The influence function is the vector of derivatives of the cutoffs with respect to a perturbation in the distribution. A general policy perturbation with score sRs\_{R} on the report distribution PRP\_{R} induces a pathwise derivative on any functional of that distribution. We find the influence function by differentiating the entire dynamic system that determines the cutoffs.

   * ‚Ä¢

     Perturbation of Primitives: The perturbation directly affects the marginal densities Hbk‚Äã(ùú∏)H\_{b}^{k}(\bm{\gamma}) that govern the path‚Äôs evolution, and the demand functions Dk‚Äã(ùú∏)D^{k}(\bm{\gamma}) that determine the stopping times. We denote their pathwise derivatives as Hbk‚Å£‚Ä≤‚Äã[sR]H\_{b}^{k\prime}[s\_{R}] and Dk‚Å£‚Ä≤‚Äã[sR]D^{k\prime}[s\_{R}]. For example, in our parametric case, the perturbation affects both the shares œÄk\pi\_{k} and the uniformity of the priority distribution.
   * ‚Ä¢

     Path Influence Function: The TTC path is defined by the ODE Œ≥2‚Ä≤‚Äã(t)‚ÄãH21‚Äã(ùú∏‚Äã(t))=Œ≥1‚Ä≤‚Äã(t)‚ÄãH12‚Äã(ùú∏‚Äã(t))\gamma^{\prime}\_{2}(t)H\_{2}^{1}(\bm{\gamma}(t))=\gamma^{\prime}\_{1}(t)H\_{1}^{2}(\bm{\gamma}(t)). We differentiate this entire equation with respect to the policy perturbation. This yields a variational equation for the influence on the path, ùùçŒ≥‚Äã(t;Ri)\bm{\psi}\_{\gamma}(t;R\_{i}), which now includes a forcing term due to the direct perturbation of the HH functions:

     |  |  |  |
     | --- | --- | --- |
     |  | dd‚Äãt‚ÄãœàŒ≥,2‚Äã(t)=J‚Äã(ùú∏‚Äã(t))‚ãÖœàŒ≥,2‚Äã(t)+F‚Äã(ùú∏‚Äã(t),sR).\frac{d}{dt}\psi\_{\gamma,2}(t)=J(\bm{\gamma}(t))\cdot\psi\_{\gamma,2}(t)+F(\bm{\gamma}(t),s\_{R}). |  |

     Here, JJ is a Jacobian term from differentiating the ODE‚Äôs coefficients with respect to ùú∏\bm{\gamma}, and the forcing term FF is a linear functional of the score sRs\_{R} that depends on the derivatives Hbk‚Å£‚Ä≤‚Äã[sR]H\_{b}^{k\prime}[s\_{R}]. The solution to this ODE gives the influence function for the path shape.
   * ‚Ä¢

     Stopping Time Influence Function: Assume school 1 fills first. The stopping time t(1)t^{(1)} is implicitly defined by the capacity constraint D1‚Äã(ùú∏‚Äã(t(1)),PR)=q1D^{1}(\bm{\gamma}(t^{(1)}),P\_{R})=q\_{1}. Differentiating this constraint with respect to the perturbation at baseline gives:

     |  |  |  |
     | --- | --- | --- |
     |  | ‚àÇD1‚àÇPR‚Äã[sR]+‚àáùú∏D1‚ãÖ(ùú∏‚Ä≤‚Äã(t0(1))‚Äãd‚Äãt(1)d‚ÄãŒ∏‚Äã[sR]+d‚Äãùú∏‚Äã(t0(1))d‚ÄãŒ∏‚Äã[sR])=0.\frac{\partial D^{1}}{\partial P\_{R}}[s\_{R}]+\nabla\_{\bm{\gamma}}D^{1}\cdot\left(\bm{\gamma}^{\prime}(t^{(1)}\_{0})\frac{dt^{(1)}}{d\theta}[s\_{R}]+\frac{d\bm{\gamma}(t^{(1)}\_{0})}{d\theta}[s\_{R}]\right)=0. |  |

     The term ‚àÇD1‚àÇPR‚Äã[sR]\frac{\partial D^{1}}{\partial P\_{R}}[s\_{R}] captures the direct effect of the perturbation on the demand functional. Solving for the derivative of the stopping time, d‚Äãt(1)d‚ÄãŒ∏‚Äã[sR]\frac{dt^{(1)}}{d\theta}[s\_{R}], yields its influence function œàt(1)‚Äã(Ri)\psi\_{t^{(1)}}(R\_{i}):

     |  |  |  |
     | --- | --- | --- |
     |  | œàt(1)‚Äã(Ri)=‚àí(d‚ÄãD1d‚Äãt|t0(1))‚àí1‚Äã(D1‚Å£‚Ä≤‚Äã[Ri]+‚àáùú∏D1‚ãÖùùçŒ≥‚Äã(t0(1);Ri)),\psi\_{t^{(1)}}(R\_{i})=-\left(\frac{dD^{1}}{dt}\bigg|\_{t^{(1)}\_{0}}\right)^{-1}\left(D^{1\prime}[R\_{i}]+\nabla\_{\bm{\gamma}}D^{1}\cdot\bm{\psi}\_{\gamma}(t^{(1)}\_{0};R\_{i})\right), |  |

     where D1‚Å£‚Ä≤‚Äã[Ri]D^{1\prime}[R\_{i}] is the representer for the pathwise derivative of the demand functional.
   * ‚Ä¢

     Cutoff Influence Functions: The influence functions for the cutoffs are found by applying the chain rule. The derivation for the second-round cutoffs simplifies considerably. As established by Leshno and Lo ([2021](https://arxiv.org/html/2510.20032v1#bib.bib31)), once school 1 fills at time t(1)t^{(1)}, its priority frontier stops advancing, i.e., Œ≥1‚Äã(t)=Œ≥1‚Äã(t(1))\gamma\_{1}(t)=\gamma\_{1}(t^{(1)}) for all t‚â•t(1)t\geq t^{(1)}.

     + ‚Äì

       The cutoff c1,2c\_{1,2}: This cutoff is defined as Œ≥1‚Äã(t(2))\gamma\_{1}(t^{(2)}). Since t(2)‚â•t(1)t^{(2)}\geq t^{(1)}, it follows that Œ≥1‚Äã(t(2))=Œ≥1‚Äã(t(1))=c1,1\gamma\_{1}(t^{(2)})=\gamma\_{1}(t^{(1)})=c\_{1,1}. Thus, we have the identity c1,2=c1,1c\_{1,2}=c\_{1,1}, which implies their influence functions must also be equal: œàc1,2‚Äã(Ri)=œàc1,1‚Äã(Ri)\psi\_{c\_{1,2}}(R\_{i})=\psi\_{c\_{1,1}}(R\_{i}).
     + ‚Äì

       The cutoff c2,2c\_{2,2}: The final cutoff, c2,2=Œ≥2‚Äã(t(2))c\_{2,2}=\gamma\_{2}(t^{(2)}), is found by solving the capacity constraint for school 2 in the residual economy. Its influence function is found by differentiating this expression: œàc2,2‚Äã(Ri)=Œ≥2‚Ä≤‚Äã(t0(2))‚Äãœàt(2)‚Äã(Ri)+œàŒ≥,2‚Äã(t0(2);Ri)\psi\_{c\_{2,2}}(R\_{i})=\gamma^{\prime}\_{2}(t^{(2)}\_{0})\psi\_{t^{(2)}}(R\_{i})+\psi\_{\gamma,2}(t^{(2)}\_{0};R\_{i}). The term œàt(2)‚Äã(Ri)\psi\_{t^{(2)}}(R\_{i}) is the influence function for the second stopping time, which is found by differentiating the capacity constraint for the residual economy. This constraint depends on the outcomes of the first round, making œàt(2)‚Äã(Ri)\psi\_{t^{(2)}}(R\_{i}) a function of the previously derived first-round influence functions.

   This more general procedure fully identifies the vector of influence functions, ùùçùêú0‚Äã(Ri)\bm{\psi}\_{\mathbf{c}\_{0}}(R\_{i}), for any perturbation to the report distribution that satisfies our regularity conditions.
4. 4.

   Total Equilibrium-Adjusted Outcome:
   The final expression is:

   |  |  |  |
   | --- | --- | --- |
   |  | Œ®itotal=Yi‚àí‚ü®‚àáùêúùí∞‚Äã(ùêú0),ùùçùêú0‚Äã(Ri)‚ü©.\Psi\_{i}^{\text{total}}=Y\_{i}-\langle\nabla\_{\mathbf{c}}\mathcal{U}(\mathbf{c}\_{0}),\bm{\psi}\_{\mathbf{c}\_{0}}(R\_{i})\rangle. |  |

   This derivation confirms that even for a complex procedural mechanism like TTC, the MPE can be constructed within our framework. The market conduct externality is fully characterized by the welfare gradient at the cutoff boundaries and the influence function of the cutoffs, which is itself found by analyzing the sensitivity of the underlying dynamic system to policy perturbations.

## Appendix D Extensions

This appendix provides formal derivations for the extensions discussed in Section [5](https://arxiv.org/html/2510.20032v1#S5 "5 Applications and Extensions ‚Ä£ Evaluating Local Policies in Centralized MarketsWe thank Kevin Chen, Ramesh Johari, Lihua Lei, Evan Munro, Davide Viviano, and Stefan Wager for their feedback, which has greatly improved the paper. We also thank seminar participants at Harvard and Stanford, as well as conference participants at the 2024 Winter Meetings of the Econometric Society, and ACIC 2025. Wisse Rutgers acknowledges financial support from the Fundaci√≥n Ram√≥n Areces, the Maria de Maeztu Unit of Excellence CEX2020-001104-M, funded by MCIN/AEI/10.13039/501100011033, and CEMFI.") of the main text.

### D.1 General Welfare Functionals

This section demonstrates that our framework extends from the mean to a general class of welfare criteria. We first provide a simple proof for functionals defined by moment conditions using the Implicit Function Theorem. We then provide a more abstract, general proof that covers any Hadamard differentiable functional.

#### D.1.1 A Direct Proof for Functionals Defined by Moment Conditions

Many common statistics, like quantiles, are defined implicitly as the solution to a moment equation. For this large class of functionals, we can prove the main result directly using the Implicit Function Theorem.

###### Proposition D.1.

Let the welfare functional ùí∞\mathcal{U} be defined implicitly as the unique solution to a moment equation ùîº‚Äã[g‚Äã(Yi,ùí∞)]=0\mathbb{E}[g(Y\_{i},\mathcal{U})]=0. Assume the function g‚Äã(y,u)g(y,u) is continuously differentiable in uu and that ùîº0‚Äã[‚àÇg‚Äã(Yi,ùí∞0)/‚àÇùí∞]‚â†0\mathbb{E}\_{0}[\partial g(Y\_{i},\mathcal{U}\_{0})/\partial\mathcal{U}]\neq 0. The Marginal Policy Effect on ùí∞\mathcal{U} is given by applying Theorem [3.1](https://arxiv.org/html/2510.20032v1#S3.Thmtheorem1 "Theorem 3.1 (The Marginal Policy Effect). ‚Ä£ 3.3 The Equilibrium-Adjusted Outcome ‚Ä£ 3 The Marginal Policy Effect ‚Ä£ Evaluating Local Policies in Centralized MarketsWe thank Kevin Chen, Ramesh Johari, Lihua Lei, Evan Munro, Davide Viviano, and Stefan Wager for their feedback, which has greatly improved the paper. We also thank seminar participants at Harvard and Stanford, as well as conference participants at the 2024 Winter Meetings of the Econometric Society, and ACIC 2025. Wisse Rutgers acknowledges financial support from the Fundaci√≥n Ram√≥n Areces, the Maria de Maeztu Unit of Excellence CEX2020-001104-M, funded by MCIN/AEI/10.13039/501100011033, and CEMFI.") (or Corollary [3.1](https://arxiv.org/html/2510.20032v1#S3.Thmcorollary1 "Corollary 3.1 (The Equilibrium-Adjusted Outcome). ‚Ä£ 3.3 The Equilibrium-Adjusted Outcome ‚Ä£ 3 The Marginal Policy Effect ‚Ä£ Evaluating Local Policies in Centralized MarketsWe thank Kevin Chen, Ramesh Johari, Lihua Lei, Evan Munro, Davide Viviano, and Stefan Wager for their feedback, which has greatly improved the paper. We also thank seminar participants at Harvard and Stanford, as well as conference participants at the 2024 Winter Meetings of the Econometric Society, and ACIC 2025. Wisse Rutgers acknowledges financial support from the Fundaci√≥n Ram√≥n Areces, the Maria de Maeztu Unit of Excellence CEX2020-001104-M, funded by MCIN/AEI/10.13039/501100011033, and CEMFI.")) to the transformed outcome Zi=I‚ÄãF‚Äã(Yi;ùí∞0)Z\_{i}=IF(Y\_{i};\mathcal{U}\_{0}), where I‚ÄãF‚Äã(y;u)=‚àí(ùîº‚Äã[‚àÇg‚àÇu])‚àí1‚Äãg‚Äã(y,u)IF(y;u)=-\left(\mathbb{E}[\frac{\partial g}{\partial u}]\right)^{-1}g(y,u) is the influence function of the functional ùí∞\mathcal{U}.

###### Proof.

Under a policy perturbation Œ∏\theta, the moment condition must hold for the perturbed value of the functional, ùí∞‚Äã(Œ∏)\mathcal{U}(\theta):

|  |  |  |
| --- | --- | --- |
|  | G‚Äã(Œ∏,ùí∞‚Äã(Œ∏)):=ùîºŒ∏‚Äã[g‚Äã(Yi,ùí∞‚Äã(Œ∏))]=0.G(\theta,\mathcal{U}(\theta)):=\mathbb{E}\_{\theta}[g(Y\_{i},\mathcal{U}(\theta))]=0. |  |

Our goal is to find the MPE, d‚Äãùí∞d‚ÄãŒ∏|Œ∏=0\frac{d\mathcal{U}}{d\theta}|\_{\theta=0}. By the Implicit Function Theorem:

|  |  |  |
| --- | --- | --- |
|  | d‚Äãùí∞d‚ÄãŒ∏|Œ∏=0=‚àí(‚àÇG‚àÇùí∞|0,ùí∞0)‚àí1‚Äã(‚àÇG‚àÇŒ∏|0,ùí∞0).\frac{d\mathcal{U}}{d\theta}\bigg|\_{\theta=0}=-\left(\frac{\partial G}{\partial\mathcal{U}}\bigg|\_{0,\mathcal{U}\_{0}}\right)^{-1}\left(\frac{\partial G}{\partial\theta}\bigg|\_{0,\mathcal{U}\_{0}}\right). |  |

The first component is ‚àÇG‚àÇùí∞=ùîº0‚Äã[‚àÇg‚Äã(Yi,ùí∞0)‚àÇùí∞]\frac{\partial G}{\partial\mathcal{U}}=\mathbb{E}\_{0}[\frac{\partial g(Y\_{i},\mathcal{U}\_{0})}{\partial\mathcal{U}}]. The second component is the MPE for the outcome variable Zig:=g‚Äã(Yi,ùí∞0)Z\_{i}^{g}:=g(Y\_{i},\mathcal{U}\_{0}):

|  |  |  |
| --- | --- | --- |
|  | ‚àÇG‚àÇŒ∏=dd‚ÄãŒ∏‚ÄãùîºŒ∏‚Äã[g‚Äã(Yi,ùí∞0)]|Œ∏=0=‚Ñí‚Äã(g),\frac{\partial G}{\partial\theta}=\frac{d}{d\theta}\mathbb{E}\_{\theta}[g(Y\_{i},\mathcal{U}\_{0})]\bigg|\_{\theta=0}=\mathcal{L}(g), |  |

where ‚Ñí‚Äã(g)\mathcal{L}(g) is the MPE operator. From Appendix [B](https://arxiv.org/html/2510.20032v1#A2 "Appendix B Derivation of the Marginal Policy Effect ‚Ä£ Evaluating Local Policies in Centralized MarketsWe thank Kevin Chen, Ramesh Johari, Lihua Lei, Evan Munro, Davide Viviano, and Stefan Wager for their feedback, which has greatly improved the paper. We also thank seminar participants at Harvard and Stanford, as well as conference participants at the 2024 Winter Meetings of the Econometric Society, and ACIC 2025. Wisse Rutgers acknowledges financial support from the Fundaci√≥n Ram√≥n Areces, the Maria de Maeztu Unit of Excellence CEX2020-001104-M, funded by MCIN/AEI/10.13039/501100011033, and CEMFI."), we know ‚Ñí‚Äã(g)=ùîº0‚Äã[Œ®ig‚ÄãsW‚Äã(Wi)]\mathcal{L}(g)=\mathbb{E}\_{0}[\Psi\_{i}^{g}s\_{W}(W\_{i})] (in the L2L\_{2} case).
Substituting these into the IFT formula gives:

|  |  |  |
| --- | --- | --- |
|  | d‚Äãùí∞d‚ÄãŒ∏=‚àí(ùîº0‚Äã[‚àÇg‚àÇùí∞])‚àí1‚Äãùîº0‚Äã[Œ®ig‚ÄãsW‚Äã(Wi)]=ùîº0‚Äã[‚àí(ùîº0‚Äã[‚àÇg‚àÇùí∞])‚àí1‚ÄãŒ®ig‚ãÖsW‚Äã(Wi)].\frac{d\mathcal{U}}{d\theta}=-\left(\mathbb{E}\_{0}\left[\frac{\partial g}{\partial\mathcal{U}}\right]\right)^{-1}\mathbb{E}\_{0}[\Psi\_{i}^{g}s\_{W}(W\_{i})]=\mathbb{E}\_{0}\left[-\left(\mathbb{E}\_{0}\left[\frac{\partial g}{\partial\mathcal{U}}\right]\right)^{-1}\Psi\_{i}^{g}\cdot s\_{W}(W\_{i})\right]. |  |

The term inside the expectation is the equilibrium-adjusted outcome for ùí∞\mathcal{U}. The influence function for ùí∞\mathcal{U} is I‚ÄãF‚Äã(Y;ùí∞0)=‚àí(ùîº0‚Äã[‚àÇg‚àÇùí∞])‚àí1‚Äãg‚Äã(Y,ùí∞0)IF(Y;\mathcal{U}\_{0})=-(\mathbb{E}\_{0}[\frac{\partial g}{\partial\mathcal{U}}])^{-1}g(Y,\mathcal{U}\_{0}). Since the construction of the externality terms in Œ®\Psi is linear, it follows that ‚àí(ùîº0‚Äã[‚àÇg‚àÇùí∞])‚àí1‚ÄãŒ®ig=Œ®iI‚ÄãF-(\mathbb{E}\_{0}[\frac{\partial g}{\partial\mathcal{U}}])^{-1}\Psi\_{i}^{g}=\Psi\_{i}^{IF}. This shows that the MPE for ùí∞\mathcal{U} is ùîº0‚Äã[Œ®iI‚ÄãF‚ÄãsW‚Äã(Wi)]\mathbb{E}\_{0}[\Psi\_{i}^{IF}s\_{W}(W\_{i})], which completes the proof.
‚àé

#### D.1.2 The General Case for Hadamard Differentiable Functionals

The result holds more generally for any Hadamard differentiable functional. The proof below uses an integration-by-parts argument that relies on the linearity of the MPE operator and an assumption of bounded support for the outcome variable.

###### Lemma D.1 (Continuity of the MPE Operator).

The MPE operator ‚Ñí‚Äã(g):=dd‚ÄãŒ∏‚ÄãùîºŒ∏‚Äã[g‚Äã(Yi)]|Œ∏=0\mathcal{L}(g):=\frac{d}{d\theta}\mathbb{E}\_{\theta}[g(Y\_{i})]|\_{\theta=0} is a continuous (bounded) linear operator on the space of bounded, measurable functions gg, equipped with the sup norm ‚Äñg‚Äñ‚àû=supy|g‚Äã(y)|||g||\_{\infty}=\sup\_{y}|g(y)|.

###### Proof.

Linearity follows from the linearity of expectations and the construction of Œ®g\Psi^{g}. For continuity, we show the operator is bounded. From Corollary [3.1](https://arxiv.org/html/2510.20032v1#S3.Thmcorollary1 "Corollary 3.1 (The Equilibrium-Adjusted Outcome). ‚Ä£ 3.3 The Equilibrium-Adjusted Outcome ‚Ä£ 3 The Marginal Policy Effect ‚Ä£ Evaluating Local Policies in Centralized MarketsWe thank Kevin Chen, Ramesh Johari, Lihua Lei, Evan Munro, Davide Viviano, and Stefan Wager for their feedback, which has greatly improved the paper. We also thank seminar participants at Harvard and Stanford, as well as conference participants at the 2024 Winter Meetings of the Econometric Society, and ACIC 2025. Wisse Rutgers acknowledges financial support from the Fundaci√≥n Ram√≥n Areces, the Maria de Maeztu Unit of Excellence CEX2020-001104-M, funded by MCIN/AEI/10.13039/501100011033, and CEMFI."), ‚Ñí‚Äã(g)=ùîº0‚Äã[Œ®g‚ÄãsW]\mathcal{L}(g)=\mathbb{E}\_{0}[\Psi^{g}s\_{W}]. By Cauchy-Schwarz, |‚Ñí‚Äã(g)|‚â§ùîº0‚Äã[(Œ®g)2]‚ãÖùîº0‚Äã[sW2]|\mathcal{L}(g)|\leq\sqrt{\mathbb{E}\_{0}[(\Psi^{g})^{2}]}\cdot\sqrt{\mathbb{E}\_{0}[s\_{W}^{2}]}. The externality terms in Œ®g\Psi^{g} are constructed via bounded linear operations on the conditional mean of gg, which is itself bounded by ‚Äñg‚Äñ‚àû||g||\_{\infty}. It follows that ùîº0‚Äã[(Œ®g)2]\sqrt{\mathbb{E}\_{0}[(\Psi^{g})^{2}]} is bounded by a constant times ‚Äñg‚Äñ‚àû||g||\_{\infty}. Thus, |‚Ñí‚Äã(g)|‚â§C‚ãÖ‚Äñg‚Äñ‚àû|\mathcal{L}(g)|\leq C\cdot||g||\_{\infty} for some constant CC.
‚àé

###### Proposition D.2.

Let ùí∞‚Äã(FY)\mathcal{U}(F\_{Y}) be a Hadamard differentiable functional with a continuously differentiable influence function I‚ÄãF‚Äã(y;FY|0)IF(y;F\_{Y|0}). Assume the outcome variable YY has bounded support. The Marginal Policy Effect on ùí∞\mathcal{U} is given by applying Theorem [3.1](https://arxiv.org/html/2510.20032v1#S3.Thmtheorem1 "Theorem 3.1 (The Marginal Policy Effect). ‚Ä£ 3.3 The Equilibrium-Adjusted Outcome ‚Ä£ 3 The Marginal Policy Effect ‚Ä£ Evaluating Local Policies in Centralized MarketsWe thank Kevin Chen, Ramesh Johari, Lihua Lei, Evan Munro, Davide Viviano, and Stefan Wager for their feedback, which has greatly improved the paper. We also thank seminar participants at Harvard and Stanford, as well as conference participants at the 2024 Winter Meetings of the Econometric Society, and ACIC 2025. Wisse Rutgers acknowledges financial support from the Fundaci√≥n Ram√≥n Areces, the Maria de Maeztu Unit of Excellence CEX2020-001104-M, funded by MCIN/AEI/10.13039/501100011033, and CEMFI.") (or Corollary [3.1](https://arxiv.org/html/2510.20032v1#S3.Thmcorollary1 "Corollary 3.1 (The Equilibrium-Adjusted Outcome). ‚Ä£ 3.3 The Equilibrium-Adjusted Outcome ‚Ä£ 3 The Marginal Policy Effect ‚Ä£ Evaluating Local Policies in Centralized MarketsWe thank Kevin Chen, Ramesh Johari, Lihua Lei, Evan Munro, Davide Viviano, and Stefan Wager for their feedback, which has greatly improved the paper. We also thank seminar participants at Harvard and Stanford, as well as conference participants at the 2024 Winter Meetings of the Econometric Society, and ACIC 2025. Wisse Rutgers acknowledges financial support from the Fundaci√≥n Ram√≥n Areces, the Maria de Maeztu Unit of Excellence CEX2020-001104-M, funded by MCIN/AEI/10.13039/501100011033, and CEMFI.")) to the transformed outcome I‚ÄãF‚Äã(Yi;FY|0)IF(Y\_{i};F\_{Y|0}).

###### Proof.

The proof establishes the identity MPE‚Äã(ùí∞)=MPE‚Äã(ùîº‚Äã[I‚ÄãF‚Äã(Y)])\text{MPE}(\mathcal{U})=\text{MPE}(\mathbb{E}[IF(Y)]).

Step 1: MPE(ùí∞\mathcal{U}) as a Stieltjes Integral.
By definition of the Hadamard derivative, the MPE of ùí∞\mathcal{U} is the integral of its influence function against the derivative of the path of outcome measures, ŒΩ‚Ä≤\nu^{\prime}. Let the bounded support of YY be [a,b][a,b].

|  |  |  |
| --- | --- | --- |
|  | MPE‚Äã(ùí∞)=‚à´abI‚ÄãF‚Äã(y;FY|0)‚ÄãùëëŒΩ‚Ä≤‚Äã(y),where‚ÄãŒΩ‚Ä≤‚Äã([a,y])=‚Ñí‚Äã(ùüè‚Äã{Y‚â§y}).\text{MPE}(\mathcal{U})=\int\_{a}^{b}IF(y;F\_{Y|0})\,d\nu^{\prime}(y),\quad\text{where}\quad\nu^{\prime}([a,y])=\mathcal{L}(\mathbf{1}\{Y\leq y\}). |  |

Step 2: Integration by Parts.
We apply the integration by parts formula for Stieltjes integrals. Let u‚Äã(y)=I‚ÄãF‚Äã(y)u(y)=IF(y) and d‚Äãv=d‚ÄãŒΩ‚Ä≤‚Äã(y)dv=d\nu^{\prime}(y), which implies v‚Äã(y)=‚Ñí‚Äã(ùüè‚Äã{Y‚â§y})v(y)=\mathcal{L}(\mathbf{1}\{Y\leq y\}).

|  |  |  |
| --- | --- | --- |
|  | MPE‚Äã(ùí∞)=[I‚ÄãF‚Äã(y)‚ãÖ‚Ñí‚Äã(ùüè‚Äã{Y‚â§y})]y=ab‚àí‚à´ab‚Ñí‚Äã(ùüè‚Äã{Y‚â§y})‚ãÖI‚ÄãF‚Ä≤‚Äã(y)‚Äãùëëy.\text{MPE}(\mathcal{U})=\left[IF(y)\cdot\mathcal{L}(\mathbf{1}\{Y\leq y\})\right]\_{y=a}^{b}-\int\_{a}^{b}\mathcal{L}(\mathbf{1}\{Y\leq y\})\cdot IF^{\prime}(y)dy. |  |

The boundary terms are zero. At y=by=b, ‚Ñí‚Äã(ùüè‚Äã{Y‚â§b})=‚Ñí‚Äã(1)=0\mathcal{L}(\mathbf{1}\{Y\leq b\})=\mathcal{L}(1)=0. At y=ay=a, ‚Ñí‚Äã(ùüè‚Äã{Y‚â§a})=‚Ñí‚Äã(0)=0\mathcal{L}(\mathbf{1}\{Y\leq a\})=\mathcal{L}(0)=0.

Step 3: Swapping Linear Operators.
We are left with the integral term. By the preceding lemma, ‚Ñí\mathcal{L} is a continuous linear operator and thus commutes with the integral:

|  |  |  |
| --- | --- | --- |
|  | MPE‚Äã(ùí∞)=‚àí‚à´ab‚Ñí‚Äã(ùüè‚Äã{Y‚â§y})‚ãÖI‚ÄãF‚Ä≤‚Äã(y)‚Äãùëëy=‚àí‚Ñí‚Äã(‚à´abI‚ÄãF‚Ä≤‚Äã(y)‚ãÖùüè‚Äã{Y‚â§y}‚Äãùëëy).\text{MPE}(\mathcal{U})=-\int\_{a}^{b}\mathcal{L}(\mathbf{1}\{Y\leq y\})\cdot IF^{\prime}(y)dy=-\mathcal{L}\left(\int\_{a}^{b}IF^{\prime}(y)\cdot\mathbf{1}\{Y\leq y\}dy\right). |  |

Step 4: Conclusion.
The inner integral, for a fixed realization of YY, is ‚à´YbI‚ÄãF‚Ä≤‚Äã(y)‚Äãùëëy=[I‚ÄãF‚Äã(y)]Yb=I‚ÄãF‚Äã(b)‚àíI‚ÄãF‚Äã(Y)\int\_{Y}^{b}IF^{\prime}(y)dy=[IF(y)]\_{Y}^{b}=IF(b)-IF(Y).
Substituting this back, the MPE is:

|  |  |  |
| --- | --- | --- |
|  | MPE‚Äã(ùí∞)=‚àí‚Ñí‚Äã(I‚ÄãF‚Äã(b)‚àíI‚ÄãF‚Äã(Y))=‚àí‚Ñí‚Äã(I‚ÄãF‚Äã(b))+‚Ñí‚Äã(I‚ÄãF‚Äã(Y)).\text{MPE}(\mathcal{U})=-\mathcal{L}\left(IF(b)-IF(Y)\right)=-\mathcal{L}(IF(b))+\mathcal{L}(IF(Y)). |  |

Since I‚ÄãF‚Äã(b)IF(b) is a constant, its MPE is zero, so ‚Ñí‚Äã(I‚ÄãF‚Äã(b))=0\mathcal{L}(IF(b))=0. This leaves the final identity:

|  |  |  |
| --- | --- | --- |
|  | MPE‚Äã(ùí∞)=‚Ñí‚Äã(I‚ÄãF‚Äã(Y))=MPE‚Äã(ùîº0‚Äã[I‚ÄãF‚Äã(Y)]).\text{MPE}(\mathcal{U})=\mathcal{L}(IF(Y))=\text{MPE}(\mathbb{E}\_{0}[IF(Y)]). |  |

This shows that computing the MPE for a general functional is equivalent to computing the MPE for the mean of its influence function, which our main framework is designed to do.
